### [230] [RREDCoT: Segment-Level Reward Redistribution for Reasoning Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06475)
*Mykyta Ielanskyi,Kajetan Schweighofer,Lukas Aichberger,Sepp Hochreiter*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Develops RREDCoT (Reward REDistribution for Chain of Thoughts) which uses the model itself to approximate optimal reward redistribution without additional generation, including analysis of segmentation strategies and state value estimation.

Result: Demonstrates advantages over MC sampling and attribution methods, and analyzes key aspects of redistribution construction including CoT segmentation and state value estimation.

Abstract: Recent advancements in reasoning language models have been driven by Reinforcement Learning (RL) fine-tuning. Most often, these rely on the Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) algorithm or modifications thereof to steer the models to produce Chain-of-Thought (CoT) traces. The final answer can only be verified, and the reward assigned, after the CoT trace is complete, making it a delayed reward problem. GRPO and its modifications correspond to Monte Carlo methods in standard RL, which are known to suffer from high variance. A possible solution to this problem is the redistribution of rewards through credit assignment, where segments of the CoT trace that are important for arriving at the desirable solution are emphasized by assigning a higher reward. While Monte Carlo sampling can be used to provide an unbiased estimate of intermediate state values, its computational overhead makes it unsuitable for train-time credit assignment in long contexts at high granularity. We introduce RREDCoT (Reward REDistribution for Chain of Thoughts), which utilizes the model itself to approximate the optimal reward redistribution without additional generation. We investigate the advantages of our method compared to MC sampling and several attribution methods. We further analyze several aspects relevant to the construction of the redistribution such as segmentation of CoT traces and state value estimation.

### [231] [Pretraining Recurrent Networks without Recurrence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06479)
*Akarsh Kumar,Phillip Isola*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: SMT replaces BPTT for training RNNs by converting it to supervised learning on memory transitions, enabling parallel training and avoiding gradient issues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard backpropagation through time (BPTT) for training RNNs has two main problems: (1) it's sequential in time, limiting parallelism, and (2) it suffers from vanishing or exploding gradients, making long-range associations difficult to learn.

Method: Supervised Memory Training (SMT) sidesteps recurrent credit propagation entirely by reducing RNN training to supervised learning on one-step memory transition labels (m_t, x_{t+1}) -> m_{t+1}. It acquires memory labels by training a Transformer-based encoder on a predictive state objective that retains only necessary information from the past to predict the future.

Result: SMT outperforms BPTT when pretraining various RNN architectures on tasks like language modeling and pixel sequence modeling. It enables time-parallel RNN training with a stable O(1) length gradient path between any two tokens without unrolling the RNN.

Conclusion: SMT enables nonlinear RNNs to better capture long-range dependencies and train in parallel, potentially unlocking the scaling of models that build temporal abstractions of past experience.

Abstract: Training recurrent neural networks (RNNs) requires assigning credit across long sequences of computations. Standard backpropagation through time (BPTT) addresses this problem poorly: it is sequential in time, limiting parallelism, and suffers from vanishing or exploding gradients, making long-range associations difficult to learn. We propose Supervised Memory Training (SMT), a method for training nonlinear RNNs that sidesteps recurrent credit propagation entirely by reducing RNN training to supervised learning on one-step memory transition labels $(m_t, x_{t+1}) \rightarrow m_{t+1}$. SMT acquires these memory labels by training a Transformer-based encoder on a predictive state objective--retaining only information from the past necessary to predict the future. By decoupling what to remember from how to update memory, SMT enables time-parallel RNN training with a stable $O(1)$ length gradient path between any two tokens--without ever unrolling the RNN. We find that SMT outperforms BPTT when pretraining various RNN architectures on tasks like language modeling and pixel sequence modeling. SMT enables nonlinear RNNs to better capture long-range dependencies and train in parallel, potentially unlocking the scaling of models that build temporal abstractions of past experience.

</details>


### [232] [Regret Minimization with Adaptive Opponents in Repeated Games](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06486)
*Mingyang Liu,Asuman Ozdaglar,Tiancheng Yu,Kaiqing Zhang*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A new regret metric called RP-Regret is proposed for adaptive repeated games, with algorithms developed to optimize it, leading to better equilibria when all players minimize it.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard external regret metrics fail to capture adaptive opponents who respond to game history, motivating a new regret measure that accounts for counterfactual reasoning in repeated games.

Method: The authors introduce RP-Regret as a game-theoretic metric that considers players' adaptive responses to history. They analyze conditions for achieving sublinear RP-Regret and propose three algorithms to address its non-convex optimization challenge: an optimization oracle approach, a linearized surrogate method, and a slow opponent adaptation approach.

Result: The study establishes necessary conditions for sublinear RP-Regret, develops three practical algorithms to minimize it, and demonstrates that when all players minimize RP-Regret, subgame perfect equilibria can be learned, leading to more cooperative outcomes with higher utilities in games like Stag-Hunt.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that RP-Regret provides a more comprehensive framework for analyzing adaptive repeated games, with practical algorithms developed to optimize this non-convex regret measure.

Abstract: In this paper, we study regret minimization in repeated games with \emph{adaptive} opponents who can respond based on histories of play. The standard metric of \emph{external regret} in online learning is known to fail to capture such adaptivity. To account for players' counterfactual reasoning, we introduce {\tt Repeated Policy Regret (RP-Regret)}, a game-theoretic metric that measures the difference between the \emph{realized} and the \emph{best-in-hindsight} accumulated utility when all players can \emph{respond} to the history of play. Compared to existing regret notions in this setting, ours is native to repeated game playing, enabling stronger comparators and opponents with fewer constraints, while maintaining the possibility of finding better equilibria when all players minimize it. We first identify necessary conditions for obtaining {\tt RP-Regret} sublinear in time, on the variation of the player's comparator strategies in the regret definition and on the memories of both the comparator and opponents' strategies. We then study additional conditions and provable algorithms to minimize {\tt RP-Regret}, which is by definition \emph{non-convex} in the strategy space. To address this challenge, we propose three algorithms: (i) one based on an optimization oracle, as assumed in some prior work in online non-convex learning; (ii) one that minimizes a convex and \emph{linearized} surrogate of {\tt RP-Regret} at each iteration; (iii) one that directly minimizes {\tt RP-Regret} when opponents change strategies slowly. Furthermore, when all players can run algorithms to minimize the {\tt RP-Regret} (or its linearized variant), certain subgame perfect equilibria of the repeated game can be learned. We also provide experiments showing that minimizing our regret notions can lead to more cooperative solutions with higher utility in games such as Stag-Hunt.

</details>

### [234] [RAINO: Anchoring Agents in Reality, A Systematic Review and Conceptual Framework for Realism in Agent-Based Modelling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05167)

Method: Systematic Literature Review.

Result: Realism is often poorly defined and lacks a consistent conceptual framework. A wide variety of methods are used to achieve and demonstrate realism, but explanations of whether and why these methods are appropriate are generally limited. The paper introduces the Reality Anchor, Input, Output (RAINO) framework.

Abstract: Realism is a central yet seemingly under-theorized concept in Agent-Based Modelling. This paper presents a Systematic Literature Review, aiming to identify how realism is currently operationalized and demonstrated. The results show that realism is often poorly defined and lacks a consistent conceptual framework. A wide variety of methods are used to achieve and demonstrate realism, but explanations of whether and why these methods are appropriate for their intended purposes are generally limited. Building on this review, we introduce the Reality Anchor, Input, Output (RAINO) framework. RAINO identifies the key structures used to argue for realism in Agent-Based Models, consisting of Reality Anchors (e.g., empirical data, formal theory, expert knowledge, common-sense expectations) and their application as model Input or Output. RAINO broadens existing perspectives on how realism is framed. It explains why different assessors may evaluate the realism of a model in different ways, and it shows how this broader framing can lead to significantly different approaches to model development.

### [236] [Learning to Contest: Decentralized Robust Fairness in Cooperative MARL via Cross-Attention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06162)
*Can Savcı*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: A decentralized policy (CAN) uses graded contention and cross-attention to infer free-riders, achieving robust fair multi-agent RL with low exploitability and high efficiency, without central allocation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fair cooperative MARL teams maximizing egalitarian welfare are exploitable by selfish free-riders; centralized allocators solve this but remove agency from agents. It was unknown whether decentralized policies could be robust, motivating the exploration of decentralized leverage under graded contention.

Method: Introduce CAN, a permutation-equivariant cross-attention policy that infers the number of free-riders from observed behavior and responds proportionally (turn-taking when none, contesting just enough when some), trained via an adversarial league (PSRO) against a range of adversaries.

Result: CAN achieves low best-response exploitability (ρ≈1.2–1.5 vs. ρ=N unprotected) across the contention range, with high efficiency (≈1.0 at D=0, 0.83–0.96 at D≥1), approaching centralized oracle performance. It outperforms fair-MARL learners (GGF/FEN, SOTO) which are either exploitable or wasteful. Robustness scales with contest leverage: strong in multi-server games, partial when leverage weakens, absent under winner-take-all.

Conclusion: Decentralized policies can be robust to free-riders under graded contention, but their effectiveness depends on the strength of contest leverage; CAN demonstrates promising decentralized allocation but has limitations in scenarios with weak leverage or when scaling to larger teams.

Abstract: Fair cooperative multi-agent RL (MARL) teams maximizing egalitarian welfare are exploitable: a single selfish agent free-rides on the surplus fair agents forgo to raise the worst-off. A centralized need-based allocator removes it, but only by taking allocation out of agents' hands; whether decentralized policies can be robust was left open. We show this futility is an artifact of all-or-nothing contention. Under graded contention (a contested resource delivers $1-c$, wasting $c$), we prove that for any $c<1$ a worst-off cooperator that contests a free-rider strictly improves on yielding, so decentralized leverage exists (Prop. 1). Realizing it is a coordination problem under uncertainty: the number of free-riders is unknown and variable, so any fixed rule is dominated. We introduce CAN, a permutation-equivariant cross-attention policy over agents' observed behaviour that infers the number of free-riders and responds proportionally: turn-taking when none, contesting just enough when some. Trained against an adversarial league (PSRO), CAN keeps best-response exploitability low ($ρ\approx1.2$-$1.5$, vs. $ρ=N$ unprotected) across the contention range, wasting almost nothing at $D=0$ (efficiency $\approx1.0$) and retaining most of it at $D\geq1$ (efficiency 0.83-0.96), approaching the centralized oracle on both axes, no central allocator. Fair-MARL learners fail on complementary axes (GGF/FEN yield and are exploitable, SOTO all-contests and wastes), while CAN is both. On two further games we find clear scope, not blanket generality: CAN stays efficient and Pareto-dominates the fair learners, but its robustness holds only in proportion to the contest leverage: strong on a multi-server game, partial when it weakens, absent under winner-take-all (Prop. 1 fails). We also report its fragilities: weak leverage and zero-shot transfer to larger teams degrade it at high contention.

</details>


### [237] [A Swarm Approach to Public Transit Using On-demand Routing in a Slime-Mold-Inspired Framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06189)
*Lindsay Burke,Maxfield Comstock,Jason Graham,Ruth Malenda,Simon Garnier,Petras Swissler*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: A distributed, slime-mold-inspired routing algorithm with dynamic transfers significantly improves demand-responsive transit performance, boosting passenger delivery rates and cutting walking time across various community densities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Demand-responsive transit (DRT) faces challenges of high operating costs and low reliability, despite being a flexible alternative to fixed-route mass-transit networks, especially in low-density communities. There is a need to improve DRT efficiency and reliability.

Method: Transition from centralized, manually-scheduled scheme to a distributed system using a slime-mold-inspired routing algorithm to dynamically route multiple vehicles, incorporating dynamic transfers and a continual cooperative bidding process for passenger allocation. Simulations were conducted on map networks from OpenStreetMap for suburban, urban, and semi-rural scenarios.

Result: The approach increased passenger delivery rates relative to a fixed-network approach by 28% in suburban, 49% in urban, and 101% in semi-rural scenarios, and achieved over 75% reduction in walking time in all cases.

Conclusion: The proposed distributed, slime-mold-inspired routing algorithm with dynamic transfers effectively mitigates high operating costs and low reliability in demand-responsive transit, significantly improving passenger delivery rates and reducing walking time across suburban, urban, and semi-rural scenarios.

Abstract: Demand-responsive transit (DRT) is a flexible alternative to traditional, fixed-route mass-transit networks. Although DRT can function well in low-density communities, high operating costs and low reliability are common issues. We propose that these issues can be mitigated by moving from a centralized, manually-scheduled scheme to a distributed system capable of dynamically routing multiple vehicles using a slime-mold-inspired routing algorithm to maximize network effectiveness. We additionally introduce the method of dynamic transfers to further optimize transit network efficiency. All passenger allocation and dynamic transfers are handled via a continual cooperative bidding process by the buses. In this paper, we present simulated results for a swarm-driven transit network in suburban, urban, and semi-rural scenarios, using map networks pulled from OpenStreetMap. We show that our approach increases passenger delivery rates relative to a fixed-network approach by 28%, 49%, and 101%, respectively, and results in over 75% reduction in walking time in all cases.

</details>
